     <p>The <b>Leland Stanford Junior University</b>, commonly referred to as <b>Stanford University</b> or <b>Stanford</b>, is an American <a href="/wiki/Private_university" title="Private university">private</a> <a href="/wiki/Research_university" title="Research university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Stanford,_California" title="Stanford, California">Stanford</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, on an 8,180-acre (3,310&nbsp;ha) campus near <a href="/wiki/Palo_Alto,_California" title="Palo Alto, California">Palo Alto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-noteLargest_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noteLargest-8"><span>[</span>note 1<span>]</span></a></sup> It is situated in the northwestern <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a>, approximately 20 miles (32&nbsp;km) northwest of <a href="/wiki/San_Jose,_California" title="San Jose, California">San Jose</a> and 37 miles (60&nbsp;km) southeast of <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-stanford_tours_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford_tours-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> It is one of the most prestigious universities in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Its most recent acceptance rate, for the Class of 2017, was the lowest ever recorded in university history.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Leland_Stanford" title="Leland Stanford">Leland Stanford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_California" title="Governor of California">Governor</a> of and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senator</a> from California and leading railroad tycoon, and his wife, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Lathrop_Stanford" title="Jane Lathrop Stanford" class="mw-redirect">Jane Lathrop Stanford</a>, founded the university in 1891 in memory of their son, <a href="/wiki/Leland_Stanford,_Jr." title="Leland Stanford, Jr.">Leland Stanford,&nbsp;Jr.</a>, who died of <a href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid" class="mw-redirect">typhoid</a> two months before his 16th birthday. The university was established as a coeducational and nondenominational institution. Tuition was free until the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> The university struggled financially after the senior Stanford's 1893 death and after much of the campus was damaged by the <a href="/wiki/1906_San_Francisco_earthquake" title="1906 San Francisco earthquake">1906 San Francisco earthquake</a>. Following World War II, Provost <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Terman" title="Frederick Terman">Frederick Terman</a> supported faculty and graduates' entrepreneurialism to build self-sufficient local industry in what would become known as <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a>. By 1970, Stanford was home to a <a href="/wiki/SLAC_National_Accelerator_Laboratory" title="SLAC National Accelerator Laboratory">linear accelerator</a>, and was one of the original four <a href="/wiki/ARPANET" title="ARPANET">ARPANET</a> nodes (precursor to the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>).</p> 
     <p>Since 1952, 52 Stanford faculty, staff, and alumni have won the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_by_university_affiliation" title="List of Nobel laureates by university affiliation">Nobel Prize</a>, including 19 current faculty members,<sup id="cite_ref-StanfordFactsFaculty_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFactsFaculty-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> and Stanford has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Turing_Award_laureates_by_university_affiliation" title="List of Turing Award laureates by university affiliation">largest number</a> of <a href="/wiki/Turing_award" title="Turing award" class="mw-redirect">Turing award</a> winners (dubbed the &quot;Nobel Prize of Computer Science&quot;) for a single institution. Stanford is the alma mater of 30 living billionaires and 17 astronauts, and it is one of the leading producers of members of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Faculty and alumni have founded many prominent companies including <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">Hewlett-Packard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nike,_Inc." title="Nike, Inc.">Nike</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sun_Microsystems" title="Sun Microsystems">Sun Microsystems</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a>, and companies founded by Stanford alumni generate more than $2.7 trillion in annual revenue, equivalent to the 10th-largest economy in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Stanford is also home to the original papers of <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university is organized into seven schools, including academic schools of <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University_School_of_Humanities_and_Sciences" title="Stanford University School of Humanities and Sciences">Humanities and Sciences</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University_School_of_Earth_Sciences" title="Stanford University School of Earth Sciences">Earth Sciences</a> as well as professional schools of <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University_School_of_Business" title="Stanford University School of Business" class="mw-redirect">Business</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Graduate_School_of_Education" title="Stanford Graduate School of Education">Education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University_School_of_Engineering" title="Stanford University School of Engineering">Engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Law_School" title="Stanford Law School">Law</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Stanford University School of Medicine">Medicine</a>. Stanford has a student body of approximately 7,000 undergraduate and 8,900 graduate students.<sup id="cite_ref-stanford_facts_2013_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford_facts_2013-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> Stanford is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a>.</p> 
     <p>Stanford competes in 34 varsity sports and is one of two private universities in the <a href="/wiki/Football_Bowl_Subdivision" title="Football Bowl Subdivision" class="mw-redirect">Division I FBS</a> <a href="/wiki/Pacific-12_Conference" title="Pacific-12 Conference">Pacific-12 Conference</a>. Stanford has won 104 NCAA championships (the second-most for a university), including at least one for each of the last 37 years, and Stanford's athletic program has won the <a href="/wiki/NACDA_Directors%27_Cup" title="NACDA Directors' Cup">NACDA Directors' Cup</a> every year since 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Stanford athletes have won medals in every <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> since 1912, winning 244 Olympic medals total, 129 of them gold. In the <a href="/wiki/2008_Beijing_Olympic_Games" title="2008 Beijing Olympic Games" class="mw-redirect">2008 Beijing Olympic Games</a>, Stanford won more Olympic medals than any other university in the United States and, in terms of total medals won, would have tied with Japan for 11th place.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <table id="toc" class="toc">

       <tr>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Stanford was founded by <a href="/wiki/Leland_Stanford" title="Leland Stanford">Leland Stanford</a>, a railroad magnate, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senator" title="United States Senator" class="mw-redirect">United States Senator</a>, and former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_California" title="Governor of California">California governor</a>, and his wife, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Stanford" title="Jane Stanford">Jane Stanford</a>. It is named in honor of their only child, <a href="/wiki/Leland_Stanford,_Jr." title="Leland Stanford, Jr.">Leland Stanford, Jr.</a>, who died in 1884 just before his 16th birthday. His parents decided to dedicate a university to their only son, and Leland Stanford told his wife, &quot;The children of California shall be our children.&quot; The Stanfords visited <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a>'s president, <a href="/wiki/Charles_William_Eliot" title="Charles William Eliot">Charles Eliot</a>, and asked how much it would cost to duplicate Harvard in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>. Eliot replied that he supposed $15 million (in 1884 dollars) would be enough.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Leland_Stanford_p1070023.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Leland_Stanford_p1070023.jpg/220px-Leland_Stanford_p1070023.jpg" width="220" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Leland_Stanford_p1070023.jpg/330px-Leland_Stanford_p1070023.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Leland_Stanford_p1070023.jpg/440px-Leland_Stanford_p1070023.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Leland_Stanford_p1070023.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Leland Stanford, the university's founder, as painted by 
        <a href="/wiki/Jean-Louis-Ernest_Meissonier" title="Jean-Louis-Ernest Meissonier">Jean-Louis-Ernest Meissonier</a> in 1881 and now on display at the 
        <a href="/wiki/Iris_%26_B._Gerald_Cantor_Center_for_Visual_Arts" title="Iris &amp; B. Gerald Cantor Center for Visual Arts">Cantor Center</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The university's founding Grant of Endowment from the Stanfords was issued in November 1885.<sup id="cite_ref-FoundingGrant_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FoundingGrant-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Besides defining the operational structure of the university, it made several specific stipulations: &quot;The Trustees ... shall have the power and it shall be their duty:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>To establish and maintain at such University an educational system, which will, if followed, fit the graduate for some useful pursuit, and to this end to cause the pupils, as easily as may be, to declare the particular calling, which, in life, they may desire to pursue; ...</li> 
      <li>To prohibit sectarian instruction, but to have taught in the University the immortality of the soul, the existence of an all-wise and benevolent Creator, and that obedience to His laws is the highest duty of man.</li> 
      <li>To have taught in the University the right and advantages of association and co-operation.</li> 
      <li>To afford equal facilities and give equal advantages in the University to both sexes.</li> 
      <li>To maintain on the Palo Alto estate a farm for instruction in agriculture in all its branches.&quot;</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>The Stanfords chose their country estate, Palo Alto Stock Farm, in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clara_County" title="Santa Clara County" class="mw-redirect">Santa Clara County</a> as the site of the university, so that the University is sometimes called &quot;the Farm&quot; to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-noteStanfordRanch_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noteStanfordRanch-28"><span>[</span>note 2<span>]</span></a></sup> The original &quot;inner quad&quot; buildings (1887?91) were designed by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Law_Olmsted" title="Frederick Law Olmsted">Frederick Law Olmsted</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Amasa_Walker" title="Francis Amasa Walker">Francis A. Walker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Allerton_Coolidge" title="Charles Allerton Coolidge" class="mw-redirect">Charles Allerton Coolidge</a>, and Leland Stanford himself.</p> 
     <p>Visiting Stanford in 1903, President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> said of the campus and the university, &quot;Now I have come to this great institution of learning and I wonder whether you yourselves fully appreciate the mere physical beauty of your surroundings. I was not prepared in the least (and I thought I was prepared for it) for the beauty of your surroundings. You have had these plans of your university made by a great architect, native to our own American soil, who himself had the sense to adapt?not to copy in servile fashion?but to adapt the old Californian architecture to the new university uses, and so we have here a great institution of learning absolutely unique, even in its outward aspect, situated in this beautiful valley with the hills in the background, under this sky, with these buildings, and if this university does not turn out the right kind of citizenship and the right kind of scholarship, I shall be more than disappointed.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In Spring 1891 the Stanfords offered the presidency of their new university to the president of <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Dickson_White" title="Andrew Dickson White">Andrew White</a>, but he declined and recommended <a href="/wiki/David_Starr_Jordan" title="David Starr Jordan">David Starr Jordan</a>, the 40-year-old president of <a href="/wiki/Indiana_University_Bloomington" title="Indiana University Bloomington">Indiana University Bloomington</a>. Jordan's educational philosophy was a good fit with the Stanfords' vision of a non-sectarian, co-educational school with a liberal arts curriculum, and he accepted the offer.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Jordan arrived at Stanford in June 1891 and immediately set about recruiting faculty for the university's planned October opening. With such a short time frame he drew heavily on his own acquaintance in academia; of the fifteen original professors, most came either from Indiana University or his alma mater Cornell. The 1891 founding professors included Robert Allardice in mathematics, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Houghton_Campbell" title="Douglas Houghton Campbell">Douglas Houghton Campbell</a> in botany, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Henry_Gilbert" title="Charles Henry Gilbert">Charles Henry Gilbert</a> in zoology, George Elliott Howard in history, Oliver Peebles Jenkins in physiology and histology, Charles David Marx in civil engineering, <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Sanford" title="Fernando Sanford">Fernando Sanford</a> in physics and John Maxson Stillman in chemistry. The total initial teaching staff numbered about 35 including instructors and lecturers.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> For the second (1892?93) school year, Jordan was able to add additional professors including Frank Angell (psychology), Leander M. Hoskins (mechanical engineering), <a href="/wiki/Walter_Miller_(philologist)" title="Walter Miller (philologist)">Walter Miller</a> (classics), George C. Price (zoology), and Arly B. Show (history). Most of these two founding groups of professors remained at Stanford until their retirement and were referred to as the &quot;Old Guard&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university officially opened on October 1, 1891 to 559 students. On the university's opening day, Founding President David Starr Jordan said to Stanford's Pioneer Class: &quot;[Stanford] is hallowed by no traditions; it is hampered by none. Its finger posts all point forward.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> and his future wife <a href="/wiki/Lou_Henry_Hoover" title="Lou Henry Hoover">Lou Henry Hoover</a> were in the first class; the Hoovers maintained close lifetime ties to the school.</p> 
     <p>The motto of Stanford University, selected by President Jordan, is &quot;<i>Die Luft der Freiheit weht.</i>&quot; Translated from the German language, this quotation from <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_von_Hutten" title="Ulrich von Hutten">Ulrich von Hutten</a> means, &quot;The wind of freedom blows.&quot; The motto was controversial during World War I, when anything in German was suspect; at that time the university disavowed that this motto was official.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coeducation">Coeducation</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Coeducation">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>The school was established as a <a href="/wiki/Coeducation" title="Coeducation" class="mw-redirect">coeducational</a> institution. However, Jane Stanford soon put a policy in place limiting enrollment of women to 500 per year, because of the large number of female students enrolling. She did not want the school to become &quot;the <a href="/wiki/Vassar_College" title="Vassar College">Vassar</a> of the West&quot; because she felt that would not be an appropriate memorial for her son. In 1933 the policy was modified to specify an undergraduate male:female ratio of 3:1.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> The &quot;Stanford ratio&quot; of 3:1 remained in place until the early 1960s. By the late 1960s the &quot;ratio&quot; was about 2:1 for undergraduates, but much more skewed at the graduate level, except in the humanities. As of 2005 the school no longer maintains a gender preference policy and undergraduate enrollment is split nearly evenly between the sexes, though males outnumber females about 2:1 at the graduate level, exclusive of Humanities.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_finances">Early finances</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early finances">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>When Leland Stanford died in 1893, the continued existence of the university was in jeopardy. A $15 million government lawsuit against Stanford's estate, combined with the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1893" title="Panic of 1893">Panic of 1893</a>, made it extremely difficult to meet expenses. Most of the Board of Trustees advised a temporary closing until finances could be sorted out. However, Jane Stanford insisted that the university remain in operation. Faced with the possibility of financial ruin for the University she took charge of financial, administrative, and development matters at the university 1893-1905; from her experience as a mother and housewife, she ran the institution as a household. For the next several years, she paid salaries out of her personal resources, even pawning her jewelry to keep the university going. When the lawsuit was finally dropped in 1895, a university holiday was declared.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Stanford_Family.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Statue_of_Stanford_Family.jpg/220px-Statue_of_Stanford_Family.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Statue_of_Stanford_Family.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Stanford_Family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Statue_of_Stanford_Family.jpg/440px-Statue_of_Stanford_Family.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Stanford_Family.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Statue of the Stanford family, by 
        <a href="/wiki/Larkin_Goldsmith_Mead" title="Larkin Goldsmith Mead">Larkin G. Mead</a> (1899)
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Stanford alumnus <a href="/wiki/George_E._Crothers" title="George E. Crothers">George E. Crothers</a> became a close adviser to Jane Stanford following his graduation from Stanford's law school in 1896.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Working with his brother Thomas (also a Stanford graduate and a lawyer), Crothers identified and corrected numerous major legal defects in the terms of the university's founding grant and successfully <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbied</a> for an amendment to the <a href="/wiki/California_Constitution" title="California Constitution" class="mw-redirect">California state constitution</a> granting Stanford an exemption from taxation on its educational property?a change which allowed Jane Stanford to donate her <a href="/wiki/Stock" title="Stock">stock</a> holdings to the university.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Edward_Alsworth_Ross" title="Edward Alsworth Ross">Edward Alsworth Ross</a> gained fame as a founding father of American sociology; in 1900 Jane Stanford fired him for radicalism and racism, unleashing a major academic freedom case.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Jane Stanford's actions were sometimes eccentric. In 1897, she directed the board of trustees &quot;that the students be taught that everyone born on earth has a soul germ, and that on its development depends much in life here and everything in Life Eternal&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-Starr1_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Starr1-43"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> She forbade students from sketching nude models in life-drawing class, banned automobiles from campus, and did not allow a hospital to be constructed so that people would not form an impression that Stanford was unhealthy. Between 1899 and 1905, she spent $3 million on a grand construction scheme building lavish memorials to the Stanford family, while university faculty and self-supporting students were living in poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-Starr1_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Starr1-43"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> However, in 1901, she transferred $30 million in assets, nearly all her remaining wealth, to the university;<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> upon her death in 1905, she left the university nearly $4 million of her remaining $7 million. In total, the Stanfords donated around $40 million in assets to the university (over $1 billion in 2010 dollars).<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:StanfordLibrary-1906.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/StanfordLibrary-1906.jpg/220px-StanfordLibrary-1906.jpg" width="220" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/StanfordLibrary-1906.jpg/330px-StanfordLibrary-1906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/StanfordLibrary-1906.jpg/440px-StanfordLibrary-1906.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:StanfordLibrary-1906.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The ruins of the unfinished Stanford Library after the 
        <a href="/wiki/1906_San_Francisco_earthquake" title="1906 San Francisco earthquake">1906 San Francisco earthquake</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/1906_San_Francisco_earthquake" title="1906 San Francisco earthquake">1906 San Francisco earthquake</a> destroyed parts of the Main Quad (including the original iteration of <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Memorial_Church" title="Stanford Memorial Church">Memorial Church</a>) as well as the gate that first marked the entrance of the school; rebuilding on a somewhat less grandiose scale began immediately. In 1931, Stanford and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> participated in the first ever intercollegiate radio debate.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Football">Football</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Football">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>From 1906 to 1919, in response to the crisis caused by numerous injuries, intercollegiate football was in jeopardy. While some colleges dropped football entirely, a few, such as the University of California and Stanford University, replaced it with English rugby. From 1906 to 1914, the two schools played rugby as their major sport, but they soon found that the objectionable practices they saw in football were introduced into rugby. Finally, when the football rules were changed, a move developed to return to football, reviving intercollegiate sports and enabling students and alumni to identify with football, an American sport.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hoover_Institution">Hoover Institution</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hoover Institution">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Institution_Library_and_Archives" title="Hoover Institution Library and Archives">Hoover Institution Library and Archives</a> (official name: Hoover Institution on War, Revolution, and Peace) at Stanford was set up in 1920 by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_C._Hoover" title="Herbert C. Hoover" class="mw-redirect">Herbert C. Hoover</a>, one of Stanford's first graduates. He had been in charge of American relief efforts in Europe after World War I before his election as <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> in 1928. Hoover's express purpose was to collect the records of contemporary history as it was happening. Hoover's helpers frequently risked their lives to rescue documentary and rare printed material, especially from countries under Nazi or Communist rule. Their many successes included the papers of <a href="/wiki/Rosa_Luxemburg" title="Rosa Luxemburg">Rosa Luxemburg</a>, the Goebbels diaries, and the records of the Russian secret police in Paris. Research institutes were also set up under Hoover's influence, though inevitably there were to be clashes between the moving force, Hoover, and the host university. In 1960, W. Glenn Campbell was appointed director and substantial budget increases soon led to corresponding increases in acquisitions and related research projects. Despite student unrest in the 1960s, the institution continued to thrive and develop closer relations with Stanford. In particular, the Chinese and Russian collections grew considerably. From the 1980s forward the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Institution" title="Hoover Institution">Hoover Institution</a> itself evolved into a conservative <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tank</a>, functioning independently from the library and archive. It continues as an integral component of the University.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-1945">Post-1945</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Post-1945">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Biology">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>The biological sciences department evolved rapidly from 1946 to 1972 as its research focus changed, due to the Cold War and other historically significant conditions external to academia. Stanford science went through three phases of experimental direction during that time. In the early 1950s the department remained fixed in the classical independent and self-directed research mode, shunning interdisciplinary collaboration and excessive government funding. Between the 1950s and mid-1960s biological research shifted focus to the molecular level. Then, from the late 1960s onward, Stanford's goal became applying research and findings toward humanistic ends. Each phase was preempted by larger social issues, such as the escalation of the Cold War, the launch of Sputnik, and public concern over <a href="/wiki/Medical_abuses" title="Medical abuses" class="mw-redirect">medical abuses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1971 professor <a href="/wiki/Philip_Zimbardo" title="Philip Zimbardo">Philip Zimbardo</a> conducted the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Prison_Experiment" title="Stanford Prison Experiment" class="mw-redirect">Stanford Prison Experiment</a>.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High_tech">High tech</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: High tech">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>A powerful sense of regional solidarity accompanied the rise of Silicon Valley. From the 1890s, the university's leaders saw its mission as service to the West and shaped the school accordingly. At the same time, the perceived exploitation of the West at the hands of eastern interests fueled booster-like attempts to build self-sufficient indigenous local industry. Thus, regionalism helped align Stanford's interests with those of the area's high-tech firms for the first fifty years of Silicon Valley's development. The distinctive regional ethos of the West during the first half of the 20th century is an ingredient of Silicon Valley's already prepared environment, an ingredient that would-be replicators ignore at their peril.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>During the 1940s and 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Terman" title="Frederick Terman">Frederick Terman</a>, as dean of engineering and provost, encouraged faculty and graduates to start their own companies. He is credited with nurturing <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">Hewlett-Packard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Varian_Associates" title="Varian Associates">Varian Associates</a>, and other high-tech firms, until what would become <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a> grew up around the Stanford campus. Terman is often called &quot;the father of Silicon Valley.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Terman encouraged <a href="/wiki/William_B._Shockley" title="William B. Shockley" class="mw-redirect">William B. Shockley</a>, co-inventor of the transistor, to return to his hometown of Palo Alto. In 1956 he established the Shockley Transistor Laboratory.<sup id="cite_ref-Carolyn_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carolyn-52"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px; display:table;"> 
      <p>The spark that set off the explosive boom of &quot;Silicon startups&quot; in Stanford Industrial Park was a personal dispute in 1957 between employees of Shockley Semiconductor and the company's namesake and founder, Nobel laureate and co-inventor of the transistor William Shockley... (His employees) formed Fairchild Semiconductor immediately following their departure... After several years, Fairchild gained its footing, becoming a formidable presence in this sector. Its founders began to leave to start companies based on their own, latest ideas and were followed on this path by their own former leading employees... The process gained momentum and what had once began in a Stanford's research park became a veritable startup avalanche... Thus, over the course of just 20 years, a mere eight of Shockley's former employees gave forth 65 new enterprises, which then went on to do the same...<sup id="cite_ref-Gregory_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory-53"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     </blockquote> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Physics">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>In 1962-70 negotiations took place between the Cambridge Electron Accelerator Laboratory (shared by <a href="/wiki/Harvard" title="Harvard" class="mw-redirect">Harvard</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Linear_Accelerator_Center" title="Stanford Linear Accelerator Center" class="mw-redirect">Stanford Linear Accelerator Center</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/US_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="US Atomic Energy Commission" class="mw-redirect">US Atomic Energy Commission</a> over the proposed 1970 construction of the Stanford Positron Electron Asymmetric Ring (SPEAR). It would be the first US electron-positron colliding beam storage ring. Paris (2001) explores the competition and cooperation between the two university laboratories and presents diagrams of the proposed facilities, charts detailing location factors, and the parameters of different project proposals between 1967 and 1970. Several rings were built in Europe during the five years that it took to obtain funding for the project, but the extensive project revisions resulted in a superior design that was quickly constructed and paved the way for Nobel Prizes in 1976 for <a href="/wiki/Burton_Richter" title="Burton Richter">Burton Richter</a> and in 1995 for <a href="/wiki/Martin_Perl" title="Martin Perl" class="mw-redirect">Martin Perl</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> During 1955-85, solid state technology research and development at Stanford University followed three waves of industrial innovation made possible by support from private corporations, mainly Bell Telephone Laboratories, Shockley Semiconductor, Fairchild Semiconductor, and Xerox PARC. In 1969 the <a href="/wiki/SRI_International" title="SRI International">Stanford Research Institute</a> operated one of the four original nodes that comprised <a href="/wiki/ARPANET" title="ARPANET">ARPANET</a>, predecessor to the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history">Recent history</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Recent history">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:James_H._Clark_Center_at_night_HDR-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/James_H._Clark_Center_at_night_HDR-2.jpg/220px-James_H._Clark_Center_at_night_HDR-2.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/James_H._Clark_Center_at_night_HDR-2.jpg/330px-James_H._Clark_Center_at_night_HDR-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/James_H._Clark_Center_at_night_HDR-2.jpg/440px-James_H._Clark_Center_at_night_HDR-2.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:James_H._Clark_Center_at_night_HDR-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The James H. Clark Center at Stanford University
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Since 2000, Stanford has expanded dramatically. In February 2012, Stanford announced the conclusion of the Stanford Challenge. In a period of five years, Stanford raised $6.2 billion, exceeding its initial goal by $2 billion, making it the most successful university fundraising campaign in history.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> The funds will go towards 103 new endowed faculty appointments, 360 graduate student research fellowships, scholarships and financial aid, and the construction or renovation of 38 campus buildings. It enabled the construction of the world's largest facility dedicated exclusively to stem cell research, an entirely new campus for the business school, added dramatically to the law school, a brand-new engineering quad, created a new art and art history building, an on-campus concert hall, a new art museum, and a planned expansion of the medical school, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2012, Stanford opened the Stanford Center at Peking University, a just-under 400,000-square-foot, three-story research center at the heart of <a href="/wiki/Peking_University" title="Peking University">Peking University</a>, consistently ranked as the best university in China. The ceremony featured remarks by U.S. Ambassador to China <a href="/wiki/Gary_Locke" title="Gary Locke">Gary Locke</a>, Stanford President <a href="/wiki/John_L._Hennessy" title="John L. Hennessy">John Hennessy</a>, and Peking University Party Chief Zhu Shanlu. Stanford became the first U.S. university to have its own building on a major Chinese University campus.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Other Stanford programs underwent notable expansion as well, such as the Stanford in Washington Program's creation of the Stanford in Washington Art Gallery in Woodley Park, Washington, D.C., and the Stanford in Florence program's move to Palazzo Capponi, a 15th-century Renaissance palace.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> The university completed the <a href="/wiki/James_H._Clark_Center" title="James H. Clark Center">James H. Clark Center</a> for interdisciplinary research in engineering and medicine in 2003, named for benefactor, co-founder of <a href="/wiki/Netscape" title="Netscape">Netscape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Graphics" title="Silicon Graphics">Silicon Graphics</a> and <a href="/wiki/WebMD" title="WebMD">WebMD</a>, and former professor of electrical engineering <a href="/wiki/James_H._Clark" title="James H. Clark">James H. Clark</a>.</p> 
     <p>In 2011, Stanford created the first PhD program in Stem Cell Science in the United States. The program will be housed at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Medical_School" title="Stanford Medical School" class="mw-redirect">Stanford Medical School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Undergraduate admission selectivity also increased, with the acceptance rate dropping from 13% for the class of 2004 to 6% for the class of 2016. Stanford's reputation, competitive admissions, and strong legacy of entrepreneurship have contributed to the East-West rivalry between Stanford and such institutions as <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>Stanford University is located on an 8,180-acre (3,310&nbsp;ha)<sup id="cite_ref-stanford_tours_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford_tours-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> campus on the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Peninsula" title="San Francisco Peninsula">San Francisco Peninsula</a>, in the northwest part of the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clara_Valley" title="Santa Clara Valley">Santa Clara Valley</a> (<a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a>) approximately 37 miles (60&nbsp;km) southeast of <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> and approximately 20 miles (32&nbsp;km) northwest of <a href="/wiki/San_Jose,_California" title="San Jose, California">San Jose</a>. The main campus is adjacent to Palo Alto, bounded by <a href="/wiki/El_Camino_Real_(California)" title="El Camino Real (California)">El Camino Real</a>, Stanford Avenue, Junipero Serra Boulevard, and <a href="/wiki/Sand_Hill_Road" title="Sand Hill Road">Sand Hill Road</a>. The university also operates at several more remote locations (<a href="/wiki/Stanford_University#Non-main_campus" title="Stanford University">see below</a>).</p> 
     <p>Stanford's main campus is a <a href="/wiki/Stanford,_California" title="Stanford, California">census-designated place</a> within <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_area" title="Unincorporated area">unincorporated</a> <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clara_County,_California" title="Santa Clara County, California">Santa Clara County</a>, although some of the university land (including the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Shopping_Center" title="Stanford Shopping Center">Stanford Shopping Center</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Research_Park" title="Stanford Research Park">Stanford Research Park</a>) is within the city limits of Palo Alto. The campus also includes much land in unincorporated <a href="/wiki/San_Mateo_County,_California" title="San Mateo County, California">San Mateo County</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/SLAC_National_Accelerator_Laboratory" title="SLAC National Accelerator Laboratory">SLAC National Accelerator Laboratory</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jasper_Ridge_Biological_Preserve" title="Jasper Ridge Biological Preserve">Jasper Ridge Biological Preserve</a>), as well as in the city limits of <a href="/wiki/Menlo_Park,_California" title="Menlo Park, California">Menlo Park</a> (Stanford Hills neighborhood), <a href="/wiki/Woodside,_California" title="Woodside, California">Woodside</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Portola_Valley,_California" title="Portola Valley, California">Portola Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> has assigned Stanford two <a href="/wiki/ZIP_codes" title="ZIP codes" class="mw-redirect">ZIP codes</a>: 94305 for campus mail and 94309 for <a href="/wiki/Post_Office_box" title="Post Office box" class="mw-redirect">P.O. box</a> mail. It lies within <a href="/wiki/Area_code_650" title="Area code 650">area code 650</a>. The university campus was listed by MSN as one of the most beautiful college campuses in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:1008px;"> 
      <div class="thumbinner"> 
       <div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Stanford_Oval_May_2011_panorama.jpg" class="image" title="View of Stanford University from across the Oval."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Stanford_Oval_May_2011_panorama.jpg/1000px-Stanford_Oval_May_2011_panorama.jpg" width="1000" height="250" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Stanford_Oval_May_2011_panorama.jpg/1500px-Stanford_Oval_May_2011_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Stanford_Oval_May_2011_panorama.jpg/2000px-Stanford_Oval_May_2011_panorama.jpg 2x" /></a>
       </div> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Stanford_Oval_May_2011_panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6b/Magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" /></a>
        </div> View of Stanford University from across the Oval.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_campus_development">History of campus development</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: History of campus development">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Stanford_Campus_Aerial_Photo.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Stanford_Campus_Aerial_Photo.JPG/300px-Stanford_Campus_Aerial_Photo.JPG" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Stanford_Campus_Aerial_Photo.JPG/450px-Stanford_Campus_Aerial_Photo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Stanford_Campus_Aerial_Photo.JPG/600px-Stanford_Campus_Aerial_Photo.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Stanford_Campus_Aerial_Photo.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> An 
        <a href="/wiki/Aerial_photography" title="Aerial photography">aerial photograph</a> of the Stanford University campus in 2008
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In the summer of 1886, when the campus was first being planned, Stanford brought the president of <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> (MIT), <a href="/wiki/Francis_Amasa_Walker" title="Francis Amasa Walker">Francis Amasa Walker</a>, and prominent <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> landscape architect <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Law_Olmsted" title="Frederick Law Olmsted">Frederick Law Olmsted</a> westward for consultations. Olmsted worked out the general concept for the campus and its buildings, rejecting a hillside site in favor of the more practical flatlands. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Allerton_Coolidge" title="Charles Allerton Coolidge" class="mw-redirect">Charles Allerton Coolidge</a> then developed this concept in the style of his late mentor, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Hobson_Richardson" title="Henry Hobson Richardson">Henry Hobson Richardson</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Richardsonian_Romanesque" title="Richardsonian Romanesque">Richardsonian Romanesque</a> style, characterized by rectangular stone buildings linked by arcades of half-circle arches. The original campus was also designed in the Spanish-colonial style common to California known as <a href="/wiki/Mission_Revival" title="Mission Revival" class="mw-redirect">Mission Revival</a>. The red tile roofs and solid <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> masonry are distinctly Californian in appearance and famously complementary to the bright blue skies common to the region, and most of the subsequently erected buildings have maintained consistent exteriors.</p> 
     <p>Much of this first construction was destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/1906_San_Francisco_earthquake" title="1906 San Francisco earthquake">1906 San Francisco earthquake</a>, but the university retains the Quad, the old Chemistry Building (which is not in use and has been <a href="/wiki/Abandonment" title="Abandonment">boarded up</a> since the 1989 <a href="/wiki/Loma_Prieta_earthquake" title="Loma Prieta earthquake" class="mw-redirect">Loma Prieta earthquake</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> and Encina Hall (the residence of <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Steinbeck" title="John Steinbeck">John Steinbeck</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Kennedy" title="Anthony Kennedy">Anthony Kennedy</a> during their times at Stanford). After the 1989 earthquake inflicted further damage, the university implemented a billion-dollar capital improvement plan to retrofit and renovate older buildings for new, up-to-date uses.<sup id="cite_ref-Rivalry_History_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rivalry_History-68"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landmarks">Landmarks</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Landmarks">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Stanford_Quad_at_night.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Stanford_Quad_at_night.jpg/220px-Stanford_Quad_at_night.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Stanford_Quad_at_night.jpg/330px-Stanford_Quad_at_night.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Stanford_Quad_at_night.jpg/440px-Stanford_Quad_at_night.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Stanford_Quad_at_night.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Stanford Main Quad at night
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Contemporary campus landmarks include the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stanford_Main_Quad&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stanford Main Quad (page does not exist)">Main Quad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Memorial_Church" title="Stanford Memorial Church">Memorial Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iris_%26_B._Gerald_Cantor_Center_for_Visual_Arts" title="Iris &amp; B. Gerald Cantor Center for Visual Arts">Cantor Center for Visual Arts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iris_%26_B._Gerald_Cantor_Center_for_Visual_Arts" title="Iris &amp; B. Gerald Cantor Center for Visual Arts">art gallery</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Mausoleum" title="Stanford Mausoleum">Stanford Mausoleum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Angel_of_Grief" title="Angel of Grief">Angel of Grief</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Tower" title="Hoover Tower">Hoover Tower</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Rodin" title="Auguste Rodin">Rodin</a> sculpture garden, the <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea_Sculpture_Garden" title="Papua New Guinea Sculpture Garden">Papua New Guinea Sculpture Garden</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Cactus_Garden" title="Arizona Cactus Garden">Arizona Cactus Garden</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University_Arboretum" title="Stanford University Arboretum">Stanford University Arboretum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_Library" title="Green Library" class="mw-redirect">Green Library</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Dish_(landmark)" title="The Dish (landmark)">the Dish</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lloyd_Wright" title="Frank Lloyd Wright">Frank Lloyd Wright</a>'s 1937 <a href="/wiki/Hanna-Honeycomb_House" title="Hanna-Honeycomb House" class="mw-redirect">Hanna-Honeycomb House</a> and the 1919 <a href="/wiki/Lou_Henry_and_Herbert_Hoover_House" title="Lou Henry and Herbert Hoover House">Lou Henry and Herbert Hoover House</a> are both listed on the <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Register" title="National Historic Register" class="mw-redirect">National Historic Register</a>.</p>
